    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2193083f1e9444eeaa87a8d786fa8996">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '57a68d02-5ea2-43b2-9eaf-738540baff1f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d17683590" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Conjugating the volt</b>: Non-conjugated compounds are shown to undergo electrochemical reaction by hydrogen-transfer-induced electron stabilization. In contrast, conjugated compounds realize energy storage through double-bond reformation.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/975cf365-9629-424b-99c7-4188588401e2/anie201801654-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/975cf365-9629-424b-99c7-4188588401e2/anie201801654-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1794ceb0-48c0-4958-a5a1-b0503fd43829/anie201801654-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/975cf365-9629-424b-99c7-4188588401e2/anie201801654-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d17683593" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Classical organic anode materials for Na-ion batteries are mostly based on conjugated carboxylate compounds, which can stabilize added electrons by the double-bond reformation mechanism. Now, 1,4-cyclohexanedicarboxylic acid (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CHDA) with a non-conjugated ring (−C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>10</sub>−) connected with carboxylates is shown to undergo electrochemical reactions with two Na ions, delivering a high charge specific capacity of 284 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (249 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles), and good rate performance. First-principles calculations indicate that hydrogen-transfer-mediated orbital conversion from antibonding π* to bonding σ stabilize two added electrons, and reactive intermediate with unpaired electron is suppressed by localization of σ-bonds and steric hindrance. An advantage of CHDA as an anode material is good reversibility and relatively constant voltage. A large variety of organic non-conjugated compounds are predicted to be promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie201801654-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIB) have recently attracted considerable attention for large-scale electrochemical energy storage because sodium involved in SIBs is naturally abundant, has low cost, and is more environmentally benign than lithium.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0001_R_d17683582e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> However, transition-metal inorganic compounds which have made great success in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) encounter many issues in SIBs, such as low specific capacity and poor kinetics owing to the larger radius and weaker binding of Na<sup>+</sup> with substrates than that of Li<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0002_R_d17683582e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Organic compounds have features such as structural diversity and flexibility and have molecular-level designability. Many organic compounds are electrochemically active in the storage of Na ions, and high specific capacity, cycling stability, and high rate capability can be achieved.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0003_R_d17683582e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> In most electroactive organic materials, redox-active functional groups (C=O,<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0004_R_d17683582e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> C=N,<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0005_R_d17683582e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> and N=O<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0006_R_d17683582e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span>) are conjugated with aromatic rings or quinone substructures (Scheme <a href="#anie201801654-fig-5001">1</a> a). This structural character has an outstanding advantage in delocalizing electrons added in a low-electronegativity atom to stabilize radical intermediates through breaking/reformation of double bonds. However, these organic materials generally show low specific capacity and uncontrolled voltage owing to the limited active redox groups and nonquantitative delocalization.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0006b" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0006b_R_d17683582e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6b - Chem. Commun.">6b</a>, <a href="#anie201801654-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0007_R_d17683582e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Mater.">7</a></sup></span> Furthermore, only a very small portion of organic compounds can be considered as the appropriate electrode materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201801654-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4b0ba5ca-3a9e-464a-8491-662255b4c510/anie201801654-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4b0ba5ca-3a9e-464a-8491-662255b4c510/anie201801654-fig-5001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/13504464-58dd-4280-aa0d-cbdb736e730b/anie201801654-fig-5001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4b0ba5ca-3a9e-464a-8491-662255b4c510/anie201801654-fig-5001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201801654-fig-5001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201801654" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The electron stabilization mechanisms in conjugated and non-conjugated organic compounds as electrodes. a) The typical conjugated and non-conjugated compounds, as well as the electronic stabilization mechanism in conjugated compounds. b) Computed molecular structure and orbitals of three non-conjugated species, HCOOH, HCOOH<sup>−</sup>, and HC(OH)<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> at the B3LYP/6–31+G(d,p) level. The dihedral angles depict the transformation degree from planar to tetrahedral structure, and cohesive energies (<i>E</i><sub>x</sub> (<i>x</i>=1, 2, 3)) depict structural stability (smaller number corresponds to more easily gaining an electron).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To address these issues, the development of a novel stabilization mechanism to extend the organic electrode from conjugated to non-conjugated compound (Scheme <a href="#anie201801654-fig-5001">1</a> a) and at the same time to accommodate more electrons is highly desired. Starting from electron occupancy in π* orbitals of C=O group, a possible strategy is to convert these π* antibonding orbitals into σ bonding orbitals localized between C and O atoms, which is favorable for the stabilization of both electrons and compounds. As shown in Scheme <a href="#anie201801654-fig-5001">1</a> b, the simplest non-conjugated molecular structure and orbitals of three species, HCOOH, HCOOH<sup>−</sup>, and HC(OH)<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> at the B3LYP/6–31+G(d,p) level are calculated to describe the transition from planar to tetrahedral structure and the orbital conversion from antibonding π* to partial σ bonding orbitals. On one hand, the single bond of C−O plays an important role in structural and orbital transitions. HC(OH)<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> with two C−O single bonds presents a larger structural and orbital transitions than HCOOH<sup>−</sup> with one C=O and one C−O. On the other hand, our calculated cohesive energies indicate that HC(OH)<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> has a lower energy cost to put electrons in a neutral molecule than HCOOH<sup>−</sup>, which is attributed to a larger portion of σ-bonding orbital formed in a lower dihedral structure. Our natural orbital analysis also confirmed different orbital distributions of different structures. As a result, it is predicted that a carboxylate (−COOH) is capable to realize electron stabilization from π* to σ through the formation of −C(OH)<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>Based on this strategy, we considered the organic compound 1,4-cyclohexanedicarboxylic acid (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CHDA), which contains a non-conjugated six-membered ring and two carboxylates in contrapositions. The structural design aims to prevent electron stabilization from double bond reformation and expects to form orbital conversion from π* to σ induced by hydrogen transfer between two neighboring molecules. Apart from electron stabilization, a non-conjugated compound generally has a larger steric hindrance to avoid radical-radical coupling effect than a planar conjugated compound.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0008_R_d17683582e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun.">8</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical characterization for CHDA sodiation was performed by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic discharge/charge measurements. To rule out the influence of the conducting agent, Super P, the electrode for CV measurement was fabricated by pressing a mixture of 90 wt % CHDA and 10 wt % polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) on Ni foam. Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0001">1</a> a shows the typical CV profiles of the CHDA electrode. During the first cathodic scan, two distinct cathodic peaks are observed at 1.18 V and 0.8 V. These peaks are ascribed to the electrochemical reduction process of carbonyl groups with two Na-ion insertion. It is accepted that Na ions can occupy the carboxylate positions to form strong ionic bonds between Na<sup>+</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0009_R_d17683582e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span> Two weak anodic peaks at 1.2 V and 1.5 V are detected. In the absence of Super P, the cathodic and weak anodic peaks observed are undoubtedly attributed to the sodiation and desodiation of CHDA, demonstrating the electrochemical activity of CHDA. The weak anodic peak feature might be due to the poor electrode kinetics induced by the low electronic conductivity, and the combination of proceeding reaction (hydrogen transfer) and electrochemical reaction, which is further explained in the following discussion. In the following cycles, the positions of cathodic and anodic peaks remain almost unchanged, indicating the good reversibility and stability. The galvanostatic discharge/charge and CV tests demonstrate that CHDA compounds with a non-conjugated structure are highly electrochemically active for storing Na ions.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201801654-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6b368ed0-b18a-442e-95f2-cbdd8fa26e22/anie201801654-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6b368ed0-b18a-442e-95f2-cbdd8fa26e22/anie201801654-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d33781ef-b3f9-4516-a0a3-ca5c9f68eed1/anie201801654-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6b368ed0-b18a-442e-95f2-cbdd8fa26e22/anie201801654-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201801654-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201801654" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Experimental evaluation of the electrochemical activity of CHDA. a) Cyclic voltammograms at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles and c) cycling stability at a current density of 0.1 Ag<sup>−1</sup>; d) rate performance at varying current densities.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0001">1</a> b displays the galvanostatic discharge/charge voltage profiles of the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 100th cycles of CHDA electrode at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range 0.01–3.0 V. These discharge curves have downslope character, which is ascribed to the formation of instable intermediate phases. CHDA delivers an initial specific capacity of approximately 1195 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a well-defined discharge plateau at 1.2 V, probably attributable to the activation of CHDA and the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Furthermore, the presence of hydrogen in carboxylic group also contributes to this long discharge plateau. As shown in the Supporting Information, Figure S1, if these two hydrogen atoms from the carboxylic group replaced by sodium atoms, the corresponding plateau is significantly shortened from approximately 500 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> to less than 50 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. A similar tendency was also observed when a hydrogen atom from the carboxylic group in terephthlaic acid (H<sub>2</sub>BDC) was replaced by a sodium atom.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0010_R_d17683582e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span> After the first cycle, a specific charge capacity of as high as 284 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved, which is much higher than that of conjugated disodium terephthalate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, 258 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) with a similar but conjugated structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0003b" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0003b_R_d17683582e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3b - Adv. Energy Mater.">3b</a></sup></span> The specific capacity of CHDA is also comparable with other organic anode materials has been reported (Supporting Information, Figure S2). Although the replacement of the hydrogen in carboxylic group by sodium can reduce the initial discharge plateau at 1.2 V and increase the coulombic efficiency, the specific capacity of the resulted sodium cyclohexane-1,4-dicarboxylate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>10</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) is much lower than that of CHDA because no hydrogen-transfer-induced electron stabilization mechanism could occur (Supporting Information, Figure S3). Negligible changes are observed in the charge and discharge profiles from the second cycle to over 100 cycles, demonstrating the stable electrochemical behavior of CHDA in charge–discharge process. A high specific capacity of approximately 249 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is maintained even after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating the excellent cycling stability of the CHDA electrode. The columbic efficiency is over 95 % after the initial several activation cycles (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0001">1</a> c). X-ray diffraction (XRD) analyses of the electrode suggest that the crystal structure of CHDA is well maintained upon sodiation and desodiation (Supporting Information, Figure S4).</p>
         
         <p>The CHDA electrode also exhibits excellent rate capability (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0001">1</a> d). During the first five cycles, the discharge capacity decreases gradually to 307 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (the 5th cycle) at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In the following cycles with gradually increased current densities, the discharge capacities of the CHDA electrode are approximately 257 (10th), 188 (15th), 151 (20th), and 125 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (25<sup>th</sup>) at 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density is decreased back to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a high reversible capacity of 277 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is recovered, demonstrating the high rate capability and structural stability. The typical discharge/charge profiles of the CHDA electrode exhibit relatively stable insertion plateau at different current densities during the discharge processed (Supporting Information, Figure S5). The results further demonstrate that non-conjugated system with unfavorable electronic conductivity still exhibits excellent rate performance owing to the conductive agent and reduced HOMO–LUMO gaps of discharge products (Supporting Information, Figure S6).<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0010_R_d17683582e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To determine electrochemical reaction mechanism, first-principles calculations were performed. Na<sup>+</sup>-occupied positions in the CHDA crystal structure and accompanied chemical reactions, corresponding to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CHDA (<i>x</i>=0, 1, 2) with different views are explored (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> a). All monomers (HOOC−C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>10</sub>−COOH) in the molecular crystal of CHDA are connected by different hydrogen bonds of −CO−H⋅⋅⋅OC− along (001) direction and CH⋅⋅⋅OC in the <i>ab</i> plane. More importantly, hydrogen transfer occurs between two monomers, forming new monomer OOC−C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>10</sub>−C(OH)<sub>2</sub>. Simultaneously, the carbon atom in −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> undertakes transition from planar to tetrahedral structures and the dihedral angle decreasing of 180°→133.8°→107.1°, whereas the carbon atom in −COO maintains the original planar structure. Na ions are migrated into the vicinity of carboxylate positions along (001) direction, the feasibility of this migration is attributed to relatively weak hydrogen bond of CH⋅⋅⋅OC between two monomers. Based on the lowest-energy structure relaxation, the first Na ion is found to occupy the vicinity of carboxylate positions along (001) direction and interact with O atoms to form common vertex Na−O tetrahedrons with strong ionic bond between Na<sup>+</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup>. Similarly, the second Na ion is inserted into symmetrical position relative to the first Na ion, interacting with O atoms to form more Na-O tetrahedrons (Supporting Information, Figure S7). The bending angle in the tetrahedral structure of −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> is increased in comparison with the first Na ion insertion. It is noted that the central six-membered ring −C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>10</sub>− does not experience distinct changes in terms of bond distances and angles, indicating no electrochemical reactions take place on these rings during the sodiation processes. Solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectrum measurements were performed to elucidate occupation positions of sodium ions in the discharged product (Supporting Information, Figure S8). Two peaks at −5.92 and 7.46 ppm indicate that sodium ions inserted into the C=O bonds in carboxyl groups and carbon anions in −C(OH)<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0011_R_d17683582e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201801654-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1cbbba01-977b-4598-a1f9-1f887555f231/anie201801654-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1cbbba01-977b-4598-a1f9-1f887555f231/anie201801654-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/45af9b1e-097a-46d6-bce4-341b10e15ce4/anie201801654-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1cbbba01-977b-4598-a1f9-1f887555f231/anie201801654-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201801654-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201801654" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Structural evolution during the discharge. Relaxed CHDA, NaCHDA, and Na<sub>2</sub>CHDA crystal structures along the (100) direction. b) Bond length changes during discharge, indicating a hydrogen transfer process. c) Bader charge analysis for carbon and oxygen atoms of CHDA, showing electrochemically active sites. d) Projected densities of states (pDOS) for the carbon and oxygen atoms in the −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> group in Na<sub>x</sub>CHDA (<i>x</i>=0,1,2). e) The valence band maximum (VBM) wavefunction distribution of CHDA and the conduction band minimum (CBM) wavefunction distributions of NaCHDA and Na<sub>2</sub>CHDA. f) Proposed hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Based on the change of Gibbs free energy, the sodiation and desodiation voltages of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CHDA (<i>x</i>=1, 2) were further calculated with the reference to Na metal.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0012_R_d17683582e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> The two Na-ion intercalation voltages are calculated to be 0.32 and 1.02 V. The calculated average voltage of 0.67 V is roughly consistent with the experimentally measured value of 0.99 V.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> b,c presents bond length and Bader charge evolutions of active sites during sodiation, and the corresponding reversible electrochemical mechanism. No obvious structural change in the non-conjugated carbon ring occurs during the entire electrochemical reaction process. The bond lengths of C<sub>1</sub>−O<sub>1</sub> and C<sub>1</sub>−O<sub>2</sub> are gradually elongated from initial (1.32 Å, 1.24 Å) to (1.39 Å, 1.37 Å) and (1.49 Å, 1.49 Å) in the first and second sodiation processes, respectively. Two O−H (O<sub>2</sub>−H<sub>2</sub>, O<sub>3</sub>−H<sub>2</sub>) bonds change from (1.57 Å, 1.03 Å) to (1.09 Å, 1.39 Å) and (0.99 Å, 1.77 Å) during the discharge process, indicating hydrogen transfer (H<sub>2</sub>) from O<sub>3</sub> to O<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, the bond lengths of C<sub>2</sub>−O<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>−O<sub>4</sub> gradually become equal (1.27 Å) to the conjugated O=C=O functional group. As a result, two different functional groups of −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> and O=C=O are formed after hydrogen transfer. According to Bader charge analysis (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> c), the carbon atom in −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> exhibits much larger electron storage with sodiation, whereas the carbon atom in O=C=O remains a constant charge. Furthermore, there are relatively small charge increase in the oxygen atoms in −C(OH)<sub>2</sub>, but not for those in O=C=O. These characterizations indicate that the −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> groups act as active redox centers for the electrochemical reactions instead of −COO groups.</p>
         
         <p>Projected densities of states (pDOS) calculations (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> d) and orbital analysis (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> e) during sodiation were carried out to reveal the detailed electron stabilization mechanism, particularly for the −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> group. This mechanism is fundamentally base for designing new non-conjugated compounds for electrochemical cells. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> d, the active π* antibonding orbitals in CHDA are constituted by major C p<sub><i>y</i></sub> and minor O p<sub><i>y</i></sub> orbitals (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> e), which are filled with electrons during sodiation, making a significant contribution to the electrochemical reactions. With the first Na ion added, π* antibonding orbitals are partially filled, forming mixed orbitals with σ bonding and π* antibonding characters. Further sodiation leads to the complete orbital change from antibonding π* to bonding σ orbital. Therefore, the orbital transition from sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized π* to sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized σ in active sites plays an important role in realizing electron stabilization, which is consistent with our prediction in Scheme <a href="#anie201801654-fig-5001">1</a>.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">By analyzing the structural change and charge distribution during sodiation, we present a hydrogen transfer mechanism for electron stabilization (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0002">2</a> f), which is obviously different from the double-bond breaking/reformation mechanism proposed for conjugated organic materials (Supporting Information, Figure S9). Generally, the whole reaction is separated into two steps:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie201801654-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/ef32fad5-e97b-4ceb-9855-724bbdeb0df3/anie201801654-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie201801654:anie201801654-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie201801654:anie201801654-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie201801654-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/dbac423d-81c3-4f28-bc27-701f238831da/anie201801654-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie201801654:anie201801654-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie201801654:anie201801654-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>It is a typical electrochemical mechanism of proceeding reaction (hydrogen transfer) coupled redox reaction, which explains CV response behavior of the cathodic plateaus and anodic peak.<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0013_R_d17683582e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> It indicates that the hydrogen transfer may be more important than Na-ion diffusion in controlling the electrochemical reaction rate.</p>
         
         <p>For illustrating the hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism in the electrochemical discharge/charge processes, <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra of the CHDA electrodes before discharge, discharged to 0.01 V, and recharged to 3.0 V were acquired (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0003">3</a> a). The peaks at 1–2.5 ppm are attributed to −CH<sub>2</sub>− in the six-membered ring. A chemical shift at approximately 12.1 ppm, characteristic of the carboxyl hydrogen is observed in the <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra of the CHDA before discharge. When the electrode was discharged to 0.01 V, this peak almost disappears and new peaks at 3.3 and 3.5 ppm attributable to −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> appear, indicating that the carboxyl hydrogen (−OCOH) tranfers to −C(OH)<sub>2</sub>. When the electrode was charged to 3.0 V, the intensity of the −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> peaks are significantly decreased. The weak recuperation of the 12.1 ppm peak after charge might be induced by the low-barrier hydrogen transfer between −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> and −OC(OH), occurring during charge and discharge.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201801654-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a1f48148-d1bf-4bc2-b56e-c27187a1294a/anie201801654-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a1f48148-d1bf-4bc2-b56e-c27187a1294a/anie201801654-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8f1c71ef-d961-4201-9526-c6601c97db67/anie201801654-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a1f48148-d1bf-4bc2-b56e-c27187a1294a/anie201801654-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201801654-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201801654" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Experimental demonstration of the hydrogen transfer mechanism. a) <sup>1</sup>H NMR and b) FTIR spectra of the CHDA electrode and those discharged to 0.01 V and recharged to 3.0 V.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>First-principles NMR calculation based on dimer model was conducted (Supporting Information, Figure S10). The <sup>1</sup>H NMR peak of −OC(OH) in CHDA is mainly distributed at 13.2 ppm, while those of −C(OH)<sub>2</sub> in discharge product are located at 3.7 and 3.9 ppm. These calculated NMR data are consistent with the experimental observation, further confirming the hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism.</p>
         
         <p>The reversible hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical mechanism was further examined by Fourier transform IR spectroscopy (FTIR). In the FTIR spectra of the discharged/recharged electrodes (Figure <a href="#anie201801654-fig-0003">3</a> b), the peak at about 1760 cm<sup>−1</sup>, characteristic of the stretching vibration of C=O bond in carboxylic acid, is weakened after discharge to 0.01 V, ascribed to the decrease in the amount of C=O bonds. These results indicate that the C=O bonds are the corresponding active sites for the sodiation. Simultaneously, a peak at 2349 cm<sup>−1</sup>, the stretching vibration of O=C=O groups, appears after sodiation and disappears after desodiation. It indicates the reversibility of hydrogen-transfer electrochemical reaction. The peaks at about 2869 and 3421 cm<sup>−1</sup> detected during discharge and charge are attributed to H−O stretching modes of −OC(OH) and alcoholic hydroxyls of the alginate binder, respectively. Our NMR and FTIR characterizations combining with DFT-calculations indicate that the insertion/extraction of Na ions within these organic non-conjugated compounds is closely related to the reversible hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemistry.</p>
         
         <p>This hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism is obviously different from classic double-bond breaking/reformation mechanism employed in a conjugated system, and thus may be extensively applied in non-conjugated organic materials. According to the hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism, we further rationally select a series of organic non-conjugated compounds (<i>cis</i>-1,3-cyclobutane dicarboxylic acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>4</sub>),<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0014_R_d17683582e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Phys. Chem.">14</a></sup></span> 2-cyclopentyl propanedioic acid (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), α,ω-alkane dicarboxylic acid (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>14</sub>O<sub>4</sub>),<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0015_R_d17683582e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a></sup></span> and adipic acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>10</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0016_R_d17683582e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Solid State Chem.">16</a></sup></span> as anode materials by first-principles calculations (Supporting Information, Figures S11–S14). CV curves of C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>10</sub>O<sub>4</sub> show distinct cathodic and anodic peaks, demonstrating the electrochemical activity of these non-conjugated organic compounds for the storage of sodium ions (Supporting Information, Figure S15). These results suggest that the hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism is applicable to a wide range of non-conjugated organic compounds. Furthermore, calculations indicate that the present hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism is also applicable to those conjugated compounds, such as 1,8-naphthalenedicarboxylic acid (C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>4</sub>),<span><sup><a href="#anie201801654-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201801654-bib-0017_R_d17683582e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Acta Crystallogr. Sect. B">17</a></sup></span> which cannot undergo double-bond breaking/reformation mechanism to realize Na-ion storage (Supporting Information, Figure S16). Therefore, based on our calculations, it is supposed that hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical mechanism is an important supplement for double bond breaking/reformation process of some organic compounds in electrochemical reaction.</p>
         
         <p>In conclusion, the non-conjugated carboxylate family offers more extensive chemical options to regulate electrochemical redox potential and capacity, which makes a significant breakthrough for the only dependence on classical conjugated carboxylate compounds as anode materials. Taking 1,4-cyclohexanedicarboxylic acid (CHDA, C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) as a good example of a non-conjugated carboxylate, our calculations indicate that electrochemical activity of carboxylate functional groups during sodiation are stimulated by hydrogen transfer between two neighbored carboxylate groups. The electronic stabilization is realized through hydrogen-transfer-mediated orbital change from antibonding π* to bonding σ, which partially suppress reactive intermediates and improve electrochemical reversibility. Our experimental characterizations in structural evolution upon sodiation and desodiation and electrochemical performance are fully consistent with the computational prediction. We open new avenues in the search for high-capacity and high-reversibility anodes by hydrogen-transfer-mediated electrochemical reaction mechanism.</p>
         
         <div class="article-section__content">
